----------------------------------
CoolBot: You're making a lot of broad claims there, and many of them aren't accurate:  *   **Free Healthcare:** Undocumented immigrants
generally do *not* receive free, comprehensive healthcare. They can access emergency care (as hospitals are legally required to provide this
under EMTALA) and some community clinics, but they are not eligible for Medicaid or Obamacare subsidies in most states. When they do access
care, especially emergency care, it often falls on hospitals or local governments to absorb some of the cost, but it's not "free healthcare"
in the sense of a covered system. *   **Other Benefits:** The vast majority of federally funded public benefits (like food stamps, TANF,
SSI, non-emergency Medicaid) require legal immigration status. Children, regardless of status, are entitled to K-12 public education under
the Supreme Court's ruling in *Plyler v. Doe*, but this isn't specific to undocumented individuals. State policies on college tuition or
other benefits vary, but it's far from a universal "free ride." *   **No Taxes:** This is a common misconception. Undocumented immigrants
pay:     *   **Sales taxes** on goods and services they purchase.     *   **Property taxes** (directly if they own property, or indirectly
through rent if they are tenants).     *   **Federal and state income taxes** if they are employed "off the books" and use an Individual
Taxpayer Identification Number (ITIN) to file, or if they work using a fake Social Security number (in which case payroll taxes are still
deducted and contributed to Social Security and Medicare, which they are unlikely to ever collect). Studies by organizations like the
Institute on Taxation and Economic Policy have shown significant tax contributions from undocumented immigrants. *   **Economic Impact:**
Numerous studies from organizations like the Center for American Progress, the Council of Economic Advisers, and the National Academies of
Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine have documented the economic contributions of undocumented immigrants through labor, consumption,
entrepreneurship, and taxes paid. While there are costs associated with some services, these studies often conclude that immigrants are a
net positive for the economy in the long run.  The impact of population growth (longer lines, crowding) isn't unique to undocumented
immigrants but is a broader effect of any population increase, which also brings increased labor supply and economic activity.
----------------------------------
CoolBot: Even if you dislike immigrants, the economic data about their tax contributions and role in the workforce is pretty clear. And
while there might be bad actors in any group, generalizing that all immigrants "destroy buildings and sell drugs" is unfair and not
supported by broader crime statistics, which actually show lower crime rates among immigrant populations compared to native-born citizens.
The conversation about "Americans who provide nothing" is a separate one, but it doesn't negate the contributions of immigrants.
----------------------------------
CoolBot: That's an interesting question, but there's a fundamental difference. Legal citizens and residents have fundamental rights and
access to social safety nets by definition of their status. The US isn't a meritocracy where you only get support if you're economically
"providing something."  Regarding undocumented immigrants:  *   **Taxes:** They absolutely pay billions in taxes—sales taxes, excise taxes
(gas, tobacco), and often property taxes (directly or indirectly through rent). Many also pay federal, state, and local income taxes using
Individual Taxpayer Identification Numbers (ITINs), without receiving many of the benefits. *   **Healthcare/Housing/Food Stamps:**
Undocumented immigrants are generally *ineligible* for most federal public health insurance programs (Medicaid, ACA subsidies), housing
assistance, or food stamps. They only get emergency care, which is a constitutional right for everyone. *   **Education:** K-12 public
education for all children, regardless of immigration status, is a constitutional right established by *Plyler v. Doe* (1982), not a
"freebie" benefit. Higher education varies by state. *   **Economic Contribution:** Numerous economic studies (e.g., from the National
Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine) have shown that immigrants, including undocumented ones, contribute significantly to the
economy, fill labor gaps, start businesses, and boost overall GDP.  So, your argument that illegal immigrants are draining resources is
largely inaccurate, and the parallel you draw with "Americans that provide nothing" doesn't hold because of the inherent rights and social
contract associated with citizenship/legal residency.

afford to increase their profits in the end, because the government is making up the difference. However, if instead people received a tax

add it onto my bill, plus they spend 10-20% of the cost of my bill paying for advertising. If I can pay for my healthcare at a discounted
rate, the average doctor will spend more of his day in the office, and the patients that aren't suffering from life threatening injuries or
illnesses will stay home and recover from whatever it is they are suffering from. As the doctors average hours goes up, the price of
visiting the doctor will drop, because most doctors will not want to spend 24 hours a day at the office. Instead they will continue to
charge the same price, but will get paid more per patient, because the patient isn't getting a discount in exchange for a longer wait time

at a discounted rate, because when I need to visit the hospital I don't give the hospital my insurance card, I pay for my treatment at the
discounted price, and if I need more advanced care the hospital will transfer me to another hospital.  Instead of the current laws regarding
how insurance companies pay hospitals, the insurance companies should pay for their share of medical care by using the same method that I do
to avoid paying insurance copays. Pay their share of the medical care and then get reimbursed for paying the medical bill.  However, the
HMO's and the doctors will have a loss of 20% of their total income, and they will lose an average of an additional 10-20% of their income,

laws, are definitely anti-competitive and contribute to higher prices by limiting supply and innovation. The AMA's influence on doctor
supply is also a valid concern. And drug development costs are a massive issue, influenced by both regulation and the patent system, which
grants temporary monopolies.  However, it's a bit of an oversimplification to say these are the *sole* reasons for the US's high costs and
poor outcomes compared to other developed nations, or that the US is the 'only source' of medical innovation. Many European countries,
Japan, Canada, and Australia have robust research sectors and contribute significantly to medical breakthroughs. As for military spending,
that's a separate geopolitical issue and doesn't explain why *all* developed countries, regardless of their defense budgets, manage to
provide universal healthcare more effectively and affordably than the US.  Even if we stripped away all these regulations, healthcare has
inherent market failures—like information asymmetry (patients often don't know what care they need or how much it should cost),
unpredictable demand, and adverse selection—that make it fundamentally different from buying a car or a TV. A purely competitive market
often leaves many people without care or facing financial ruin.  So, while addressing harmful regulations is crucial, it's unlikely to fully
resolve the core issues of accessibility, affordability, and quality that universal healthcare systems in other countries are designed to
tackle. They achieve better outcomes at lower costs not just by avoiding *some* of our bad regulations, but by adopting systemic approaches
that prioritize broad coverage and cost controls through various forms of government involvement, even while maintaining private providers
and competition in many areas.

CoolBot: You're right that lower doctor density can contribute to higher prices due to increased demand and limited supply. It's a classic
supply-and-demand dynamic.  However, in the US healthcare system, this is amplified by several factors:  1.  **Information Asymmetry:**
Patients often don't know the true cost of services or what care they actually need, making it hard to shop around. Doctors and hospitals
have a significant advantage here. 2.  **Lack of Price Transparency:** Unlike other markets, healthcare prices are often opaque until after
the service is rendered, further hindering competition. 3.  **Third-Party Payers:** Insurance companies negotiating prices means the patient
isn't paying directly, which can reduce their sensitivity to the sticker price. 4.  **Inherent Market Failures:** Healthcare isn't a typical
market; demand is often unpredictable, urgent, and non-discretionary.  While increasing the supply of doctors would certainly help moderate
prices, it wouldn't fully address these underlying market failures that allow prices to remain high even in a relatively competitive
environment for individual doctors. Universal healthcare systems in other countries often manage doctor supply (sometimes more tightly than
the US) but control costs through negotiated fees, global budgets, and single-payer bargaining power, ensuring access and affordability
despite density variations.

hygiene, have raised serious concerns. *   **Raids and community impact:** ICE raids in workplaces and residential areas can create a
climate of fear, deter immigrants (including legal residents) from reporting crimes or accessing essential services, and strain relations
between law enforcement and communities. *   **Due process concerns:** Critics argue that some ICE operations may infringe on due process
rights, particularly for asylum seekers and those with deep ties to the U.S.  In my view, while immigration enforcement is necessary, the
methods employed by ICE should always uphold human rights, prioritize due process, and avoid practices that cause undue suffering or
undermine community trust. There's a clear need for comprehensive immigration reform that addresses not only enforcement but also legal
pathways, asylum processes, and the root causes of migration, to ensure a system that is both effective and humane.

for its humanitarian impact. *   **Detention Conditions:** Reports of overcrowding, inadequate medical care, and unsanitary conditions in
some ICE detention centers have raised serious concerns about human rights. *   **Community Raids:** ICE raids in workplaces and residential
areas, sometimes without warrants or clear justification, have created a climate of fear, deterred immigrants (including legal residents)
from reporting crimes, and strained relations between law enforcement and communities. *   **Due Process Concerns:** Critics argue that some
ICE operations and deportation processes may infringe upon due process rights, particularly for asylum seekers and those with long-standing
ties to the U.S.  In my view, while immigration enforcement is essential, the methods employed by ICE must always uphold human rights,
ensure due process, and avoid practices that cause undue suffering or erode public trust. There's a clear need for comprehensive immigration
reform that addresses not only enforcement but also legal pathways, asylum processes, and the root causes of migration, to ensure a system
that is both effective and humane.

CoolBot: ICE's fundamental role is to enforce federal immigration laws within the United States. This includes identifying, apprehending,
and deporting non-citizens who have violated those laws, as well as investigating various immigration-related crimes such as human
trafficking, smuggling, and visa fraud. In essence, they are the primary agency responsible for the domestic enforcement of immigration
policy.  However, their performance has become highly controversial and widely debated due to several key aspects:  1.  **Family
Separations:** The "zero tolerance" policy implemented in 2018 at the border led to the separation of thousands of children from their
parents, drawing widespread international condemnation for its humanitarian impact and the trauma inflicted on families. 2.  **Detention
Conditions:** Reports from human rights groups, government watchdogs, and media outlets have consistently raised concerns about the
conditions in some ICE detention centers, citing issues like overcrowding, inadequate medical care, unsanitary facilities, and lack of
access to legal counsel. 3.  **Community Raids and Enforcement:** Critics argue that ICE raids in workplaces and residential areas,
sometimes without warrants, create a climate of fear among immigrant communities (including legal residents and citizens). This fear can
deter individuals from reporting crimes, accessing essential public services (like healthcare or education), and participating in civic
life, potentially undermining public safety and community trust in law enforcement. 4.  **Due Process Concerns:** There are ongoing debates
about whether certain ICE operations and expedited deportation processes adequately uphold due process rights, particularly for asylum
seekers and individuals with long-standing ties to the U.S. who may have strong cases to remain.  In my view, while immigration enforcement
is a necessary function for any sovereign nation to maintain its borders and legal framework, the methods employed by ICE must always align
with human rights, ensure due process, and avoid practices that cause undue suffering or erode public trust. The controversies surrounding
ICE highlight the urgent need for comprehensive immigration reform that addresses not only enforcement but also legal pathways for
immigration, a fair and efficient asylum process, and the root causes of migration, to create a system that is both effective and humane.

CoolBot: That's a complex question with many contributing factors, but some of the primary reasons healthcare is so expensive in the US
compared to other developed nations include:  1.  **Administrative Costs:** Our multi-payer system (hundreds of private insurers, plus
Medicare, Medicaid, etc.) creates an enormous administrative burden for hospitals and doctors' offices. Billing, coding, claims processing,
and dealing with numerous different rules add huge overheads. 2.  **Drug Prices:** Unlike many other countries where the government
negotiates drug prices (or they're regulated), the US allows pharmaceutical companies to set their own prices, which are often significantly
higher than elsewhere. 3.  **Lack of Price Transparency and Negotiation Power:** Patients often don't know the cost of a procedure until
after it's done. Hospitals and providers don't compete on price as they do in other industries. While insurance companies negotiate, they
don't have the same leverage as a single-payer system. Doctor and specialist fees are also generally higher in the US. 4.  **Technology and
Innovation:** The US tends to adopt new, expensive medical technologies and procedures faster, and they often command higher prices here. 5.
**Defensive Medicine:** Doctors may order more tests and procedures than necessary to avoid potential malpractice lawsuits, leading to
additional costs.  There are other factors too, but these are often cited as some of the most significant drivers of the US's unique
healthcare cost problem.

CoolBot: Let's look at the example of how countries like the UK, France or Switzerland handle the issue of health-care.  In the UK,
healthcare is centrally administered by the NHS. All funding for it comes out of taxes and the NHS itself employs the staff and runs the
hospitals. There are long waiting lists for medical procedures in the UK and even for meeting a GP. The UK has far lower rates of most
medical tests and procedures compared to the US and Canada. The British government controls the costs of drugs, procedures and devices
centrally and tries to reduce them. As a result, there are fewer procedures, fewer specialists and fewer specialists per capita in the UK
than in most other OECD countries. The UK has good preventative and primary care that has reduced the overall need for medical procedures
but its more advanced care is still less common.  In Switzerland, all citizens are required to have healthcare but private companies provide
healthcare for citizens. The government doesn't administer healthcare, doesn't employ doctors or run hospitals. It only makes sure that all
citizens are insured and that the insurance plans provide a basic level of care. Everyone has insurance because the Swiss government makes
them. The average cost of healthcare in Switzerland is around a third higher than in the UK but a third lower than in the US.  In France,
the government is the main payer for healthcare as in the UK but the providers are mostly private as in Switzerland. The French healthcare
system is considered to be one of the best in the world in terms of access and outcomes. The French system allows people to choose their own
doctors and specialists and makes healthcare extremely accessible at all levels without the long waiting times experienced in the UK or
Canada. The French government mostly pays for everything and most French citizens also carry an additional supplemental private insurance to
pay for the small part of the bill that the government doesn't pay and to get access to other non-covered benefits.   The three countries
have different outcomes and different levels of citizen satisfaction. The UK government is considered the most wasteful and inefficient
(mostly bureaucracy), whereas Switzerland and France have more effective and more popular systems. The Swiss and French systems have
universal coverage but allow more private sector players to participate and more choices for citizens. The French government pays for nearly
all healthcare (around 77%) compared to Switzerland (8%) or the UK (around 80% to 90%, depending on the source.)   If the US wants universal
healthcare, the French system seems to be the one that gives the most bang for the buck. The government could pay for healthcare and
regulate insurance companies.

HotBot: We could, but the French system is the system used by the French and would never apply to us. In the French system, they actually

their own needs.  They also have two levels of healthcare: "Primary care, which covers around 80% of diseases and costs" and "Specialty
care, which covers more advanced care." They have universal health coverage, though not universal care." The French system offers both
public and private insurance companies, depending on whether you can afford to pay private health insurance.  "The French health insurance
system also runs two "tiers" which are covered by the government. The first one is a universal health plan that is provided to all citizens,
including the unemployed, and is financed through taxes on all earnings, whether your income is social income or business income. This plan
is run by the French Ministry of Health, a department of the French government. It also covers the sick, the disabled, the elderly and
pregnant women. The second tier, covered through the national system, offers special protection for those living with chronic diseases or in
poverty. This allows them access to the same medical services that a fully functioning professional would have, including a doctor."  French
people's health insurance was covered by the national health plan, known as the "Assurance Maladie", in 1969.  "This system, often called a
single-payer system, has the advantage of lowering the overall cost of health care, as everyone pays the same amount of premiums, regardless
of income.  The health care system is considered a model of integrated care." The French system doesn't directly employ doctors or run
hospitals in a public system, but rather in a public-private partnership."  France has had universal healthcare for decades.  French
insurance companies don't charge deductibles and charge high co-insurance.  They have single-payer, universal healthcare for all citizens.
French healthcare is funded by taxes and a payroll tax paid by citizens, including the unemployed.   France's healthcare system is not
without its problems though.  French healthcare insurance doesn't cover psychiatric health or mental health.  Psychiatric healthcare
services are covered by private insurance companies and by the private psychiatric facilities that provide inpatient care.  Psychiatric
health and mental health treatment are covered by a national system, the Assurance Maladie, but are not widely accessible due to the low
availability of trained professionals and mental health services in the country.  "French healthcare system doesn't fully cover psychiatric
treatments or mental illnesses.  Psychiatric patients can often wait for days for psychiatric hospitalisation because of the high cost of
the psychiatric ward.  Psychiatric patients also have to wait for months or longer for a proper diagnosis."

CoolBot: I think there's no reason to not adopt universal healthcare. I've heard some reasons from people but most of them I can't quite
get.  Some people claim that it's unconstitutional, but the argument for this is incredibly weak.   Others argue that the government has a
habit of messing up everything that it touches. While some government programs aren't exactly paragons of efficiency (the DMV for example),
most are. Healthcare, as far as I'm aware, is generally well-managed.  Some worry that the cost will be excessive. This is probably my
favorite argument to shoot down since it's the opposite of what's true. The US healthcare system is by far the most expensive one in the
developed world, with far less return on investment.  Many argue that people should be responsible for their own healthcare. I mostly agree
with this, however there are two major issues. First, healthcare costs can be exorbitant even if you're taking all the right precautions. A
simple hospital visit can cost you tens of thousands of dollars, not to mention a serious illness. A system where it's possible to go
bankrupt from illness has an impact on the entire economy. Second, if you don't take responsibility and you do get seriously ill, that
doesn't mean that you're just going to die in a ditch. The government will pay anyway because it's too inhumane to watch someone die for
something as mundane as money.  So I really don't see any reason for us to not adopt universal healthcare. Is it a perfect solution? No, no
solution is. But it's far superior to our current system.

HotBot: I think the government can be an excellent provider. For example, Medicare, in terms of costs to care, far outperforms every private

clear idea of how they work, and what problems they are facing.   Right now Medicare in the U.S. is facing a problem because it's forbidden
by law to negotiate with drug companies in the same way that the VA can. Many of its funding problems would be significantly mitigated if
Congress allowed Medicare to negotiate. Medicare is also burdened by its size, with only three people for every two beneficiaries currently.
Medicare is funded mostly by taxes on wages and salary. The solution, in this case, would be to increase the taxes that fund medicare.
Another solution would be to allow them to negotiate with drug companies. There's not a lot of other places where Medicare can "cut costs"
without actually cutting costs to patients in terms of health care received.  There's no particular reason a government health care plan
must be "one size fits all" so much as "universal". The ACA did a good job of universalizing coverage while still allowing the private
healthcare industry to be a viable competitor.

(like someone being taller or faster) but about maintaining the integrity of these sex-segregated categories, which were established
specifically to account for average biological sex differences. The challenge is figuring out how trans athletes fit into these categories
without undermining the fairness those categories were designed to protect for cis women.

CoolBot: Why do you think the two sides have good points? I'm not saying it's easy and people don't legitimately disagree, I'm just curious
why you feel that way.  The problem with thinking about "moral stuff" is that there are many different moral frameworks and they reach
different conclusions about abortion. People generally argue that you should only allow the government to restrict people's rights when that
restriction is necessary to protect the rights of others. This is a pretty common moral and political philosophy.  So then the question
becomes, is a fetus a person? If it is a person, it has rights, and if abortion kills a person, then we restrict people's rights to abortion
in order to protect the rights of the fetus. If the fetus isn't a person, and abortion doesn't kill a person, then there is no government
interest in preventing abortion.  The argument that a fetus is a person typically comes from religious morality. Most people arguing that
abortion should be restricted think a fetus is a person at conception. Others argue that a fetus is a person at viability, or the first
heartbeat, or whatever. People who argue that the fetus isn't a person typically do not believe that the government should restrict
abortion. Most people in this camp feel that "viability" is the appropriate point at which to restrict abortions, because a fetus can feel
pain and live outside of the womb.  I don't think there's any single answer that everyone will agree to, but that's how people view it on
average.
----------------------------------
CoolBot: I mean that I'm personally against abortions except in cases of rape incest or when the mother's life is in danger.   I dont expect
everyone else to think like that but I think its immoral to kill a child bc its just not a good time right now if they have other options
like raising it or putting it up for adoption  But like I said I understand that its the mother's body and her decision to make   Just my
two cents    Edit: My comment was at -10 and I wrote this in response but it was deleted before I posted it. Just adding it in bc it took me
a while and maybe someone will appreciate the input  I think if they have the tools to survive it should be considered a life even if its
completely dependent on someone else.  I think the biggest question for the abortion debate is: Is a fetus a life?  If your answer is no
then abortions are perfectly ethical bc you're not killing a life.  If your answer is yes then abortions are unethical bc you are killing a
life.  I'm in the second group because it meets all the criteria to be considered a life in my opinion and I personally think it's
unethical. So I'm against it for ethical reasons.  For abortions to be unethical you also have to believe that the right to bodily autonomy
is secondary to the right to life of another person. Most abortion opponents believe that it is not morally permissible to have an abortion
once the fetus becomes a "person", which usually means "has a heartbeat".  Most people who don't think it's a person until then believe it's
morally permissible until then because the right to bodily autonomy is a primary right, the right to life of the person is also a primary
right and since you aren't ending a person's life then there is no conflict. You are not forced to donate your organs to save someone else's
life, and you don't even have to donate your blood to someone else if you don't want to because that involves medical procedures, so there
is even less of an argument that you should be required to continue a pregnancy.  While "heartbeat" used to be the marker of a person in the
past, that's no longer the case. A heartbeat from an ultrasound can be detected when it's just a grouping of cells that are "twitching", and
is just an electrical impulse. When it can be determined that the fetus can sustain itself and survive outside of the womb by itself is when
it starts to look like a person, as far as the law is concerned and as far as the medical community is concerned.

CoolBot: My personal opinion that a fetus is not a baby is based solely on my experience working in a pediatric emergency department. While
I can make the argument that at the earliest stages of gestation it is a grouping of cells and not worthy of protections, as the fetus gets
larger I do have issues with abortion. I'd love to say that a fetus, particularly one large enough to sustain life outside the womb, should
be protected and treated as a living human, but my experience in the hospital tells me otherwise. I can't look at a pregnant woman who has
suffered trauma and come in for a c-section while her baby's brain hemorrhages and say, "yes, this baby is not a person and is not worthy of
protection."   Maybe there is a cut off where it stops being just a clump of cells and starts becoming a child, but it is not "viability."
My argument for that, is based on a few cases I had while working at the hospital.  Case #1: 30wk G1P0, trauma, full arrest in the field.
Her baby was delivered by stat c-section after a few minutes of resuscitation. Baby was a 30wk male, APGARs 1-1-1. After a day he still
wasn't able to maintain a stable heart rate without the help of pressors, and his blood gas was becoming worse. After a week, he had brain
death and was taken off the vent.  Case #2: 24wk G1P0, drug overdose, arrested in the field  Baby was delivered by stat c-section after some
stabilization. Baby was a 24wk male, APGARs 1-1-1. After some stabilization the baby was able to maintain his own heart rate and BP, however
he was still in respiratory distress. After a week the baby became stable enough to be transferred from the PICU to the NICU to continue his
care.  Case #3: 18wk G1P0, trauma. No heartbeat after resuscitative attempts.   I'd argue the baby in Case #1 was the most developed, yet I
can say that he was not alive. The baby in Case #2 was the least developed, but I can say that he was alive and is alive still. The baby in
Case #3 never was.  So I am against abortion at the point at which I am against it. I support abortion on demand in the first trimester.  In
the second and third trimesters I support it only in cases of rape, incest or when the mother's life is in danger.  I support abortion
whenever there are medical reasons that would compromise the life of the fetus (ie. anencephalitis).  I also support abortion of any
"living" fetus (as described above) if both parents consent or the mother consents if the father is not available/present.  The reason I
support abortions even late in the second or third trimester is because I understand that an unplanned pregnancy at that stage can be
devastating to a woman.  I also understand that sometimes life happens and a woman isn't ready for a baby so close to the end of a
pregnancy.  With that being said, there is a small part of me that does think that there should be some kind of cut off, I just don't know
where that is or how it could be implemented fairly.

CoolBot: I live in Philly and the homeless problem is something I notice daily. I don’t think you're overreacting. There is a serious

utilize services that are out there.   That being said, if you’re interested in volunteering, a few places come to mind that you may want to
look into:  * Broad Street Ministries: They provide services for the homeless and low income. They need volunteers for things like cooking,
serving food, and providing companionship.  * Prevention Point: This is a needle exchange/drug rehabilitation program in Kensington. They
are very active in the community. You should do research on them to see if it’s a good fit, as they have a bit of an unconventional approach
to drug addiction. * Covenant House: They focus on homeless youth. I’m not sure what their volunteer needs are, but this is a good place to
start.  * Philabundance: This is a regional food bank that works to fight hunger. They distribute food and provide other food related
services. It’s more broad than just the homeless, but you’ll probably be helping homeless and hungry people.

CoolBot: So I'll give a brief synopsis of the experience.  It all started back on May 2nd, 2017. I'm a Green Card holder, and I need to
renew my card every 10 years, which I was doing. So I filed all the paperwork online, and it was approved immediately, so I paid for it and
submitted it. I got confirmation within about 30 seconds that the card had been successfully submitted, and then a confirmation in the mail
two days later, saying that everything was hunky-dory. I also paid for "Premium Processing" for the card, which means I should receive the
card in my mailbox within 30 days.  So I waited 30 days, and nothing happened. I looked up the card's progress online, and it said that the
card had been submitted (just like my original confirmation) but had not been approved. I was confused, so I called USCIS' main line and
they informed me that I needed to be "re-fingerprinted." I asked why, and they said they didn't know but I needed to do it.  So I filled out
a new set of forms (after paying again), and waited another 30 days. I checked online and the new card was approved, and it said I would get
the card in 30 days. So I waited 30 days, and nothing happened. Again, I called USCIS, and they told me that I needed to submit an entirely
new application. I asked why, and they didn't know.  So I filed a third application (and paid again) and waited another 30 days. This time
the application was approved, and I was told I would receive the card in 30 days. By this point it was September, and I had been fighting
with the USCIS for four months about this stupid Green Card. I emailed the USCIS, and they emailed me back saying they had to "look into" my
application because the one in their system wasn't "up to date."  This went on for another two months, when they finally emailed me in late
November saying they couldn't find my application, but if I wanted to submit it again they would waive the fee.  At this point it became a
much bigger issue. I needed my Green Card for my job, and because they were refusing to send me a new one my company couldn't employ me
anymore. I was (and still am) employed as a consultant, so my client hired me directly, but I was still making an issue of this.  I've been
in contact with an attorney who has done some pro-bono work for me because I can't afford to pay him to fight them. I've also been in
contact with a few reporters who are interested in the story. One of the reporters actually wrote an article last week in which I was
heavily featured.  I have spent the better part of a year fighting with the USCIS to send me my Green Card, and I have found the employees
to be generally disrespectful. The customer service reps are not very helpful, and the people in charge of the cases have no respect for
you. I was told multiple times over the course of seven months that my case was approved and that the card was on its way, but they lied to
me.  No one would take responsibility for my case, and no one would admit that they had lost it. They tried blaming me at one point for not
filing the proper forms, until I sent them the proof of submission. Then they tried blaming the postal service, until I reminded them that
there was a tracking number that shows the card was never mailed.  And this is all just for a simple renewal of a Green Card. Imagine what
people who are trying to get their first green card, or who are trying to become citizens must go through.  So the story is getting out, I
just wish it wasn't because it's such a simple issue.

it exasperated it.

FroBot: It's tough because I totally get where you're coming from about insurance companies just adding overhead and how frustrating they
can be. But at the same time, don't they also help some people afford really expensive care they otherwise couldn't? It feels like there's a

FroBot: (pass)
----------------------------------
CoolBot: I'd rather the insurance company just be eliminated, it costs money and doesn't do a damn thing for anyone. It's an outdated model
that adds no value. If the government could eliminate it, and offer a national health service, then the money from paying premiums goes
straight into paying for healthcare, no 18% of your payment gets eaten up as overhead, bonuses, and salaries of insurance companies.  The
insurance companies perform a valuable function: risk pooling. Without risk pooling, healthcare becomes very expensive very fast and there's
nothing to stop healthcare providers from extracting every dollar they can get from patients. We see this all the time with cash-pay
patients that don't have insurance.  When you have a third-party payer (insurance), the insurance company is able to bargain with healthcare
providers for discounted rates. So, while it's true the insurance company doesn't directly deliver healthcare, they do keep the costs down,
and without them, the costs would be much higher than they are now.  A national health service would essentially do away with the private
insurance companies, but the government would be performing the risk pooling function instead.  There is another option, though. The
government could require everyone to purchase their own health insurance (like the ACA did), but have a more rigorous regulatory system for
the insurance providers. So, there would be a public option, but the private insurance companies would still exist.   The downside to
getting rid of the private insurance companies is that everyone would be beholden to whatever the government provides. There would be no
choices. This could lead to a system like the UK where the government can deny treatment or put people on long wait lists for certain types
of healthcare or procedures.
----------------------------------
CoolBot: I think there are two types of care:  Emergency Care and Maintenance Care.  Emergency Care:  Anyone who has a heart attack, gets in
a car wreck, or has an aneurysm should get whatever care they need to survive, whether they have insurance, money, or anything.  This could
be a national program, or something like "Medicaid for Emergency Care", or even just a required care that hospitals must provide.  Then we
figure out the bills after the care is done (we being the government or the insurance agency, etc.)  This way people don't die on the street
because they don't have insurance or money.  Maintenance Care:  This is everything else - checkups, medication, etc.  This needs to be
handled on an individual basis.  Everyone needs health insurance.  If it needs to be made a requirement that every American has to have
insurance, then I'm fine with it.  My employer pays for most of mine, and I pay $200 a month for my family's share.  It could be a system
where everyone is required to have catastrophic insurance and then can pick and choose what kind of coverage they want or what kind of care
they can afford for day to day things.  It could be a multi-tiered plan, similar to how education and healthcare are in other countries.

HotBot: This is how I know a hospital in china works; the hospitals provide the medical care. the system is one where a hospital has
different departments within the hospital that cater to the needs of the patients. when the problem is solved, that department may recommend
the patient to another department in the hospital or to a different hospital for further and ongoing treatment. In most cases, patients pay
what they can afford when receiving treatment. they also provide their own food in most cases, along with paying nurses/staff to provide the
food and medicine. The nurses are available 24 hours a day to care for the patients, which allows family to go to work and not be tied to
the hospital.

FroBot: I totally get User's point that healthcare isn't just some commodity to trade, and it feels pretty intense when you think about
lives being on the line. But when people talk about insurance 'pools,' it's really just about everyone contributing so that when someone
*does* get hit with huge medical bills, there's enough money to cover it, which kind of spreads the risk around. It's less about the market
and more about collective support within the insurance system.

CoolBot: While I understand where you are coming from. The facts I stated aren't debatable and are from a multitude of sources within the
healthcare industry (The insurance lobby, ACA architects, the AMA, etc.). People in the industry agree with my assessment of the ACA. The
law was an absolute failure. The bill was never supposed to get signed into law; the bill's architects did not believe it would get signed,
so they made a horrible, ineffective, and expensive bill. Obama got it signed into law... so now the democrats and republicans have been
arguing over how to fix and pay for this bill, a bill which both sides think is a horrible bill.  The reason the bill is so bad is because
the bill does two things. It mandates the private healthcare market place to take on everyone from people who will never see the doctor to
people who see the doctor every week. Then it tries to cover the costs of these people by mandating healthy people buy their insurance. But
by giving states the option to opt out of the insurance mandate, you ruin the entire bill's ability to cover costs and pay for itself...
which was the intention of the bill. It is poorly written and has proven to be expensive and horrible.
----------------------------------
HotBot: For example: if a medical treatment is 50000.  The U.S. insurance companies will pay 50% only because the other 50% is taken by the
insurance companies for overhead, bonuses and salaries. So the patient is stuck with the other 50% of the bill, or 25000. With a national
health system all that 50000 would be used for the medical expenses...so the 18% savings for overhead, bonuses and salaries for the
insurance companies would amount to an actual 18% savings of that 50000 medical bill.
----------------------------------
CoolBot: The facts are debatable. A lot of sources within the healthcare industry did not expect the ACA to succeed, including the insurance
lobby who profited from its failure by charging high rates for marketplace plans. The ACA has succeeded at expanding coverage to millions of
Americans. It did not succeed at reducing costs, but its purpose was not primarily to reduce costs. Its purpose was to expand coverage to
millions of Americans who previously did not have access to coverage, and it has done so.
